    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7305715742817420300">'The Wall', the lead story in this collection, introduces three political prisoners on the night prior to their execution. Through the gaze of an impartial doctor--seemingly there for the men's solace--their mental descent is charted in exquisite, often harrowing detail. And as the morning draws inexorably closer, the men cross the psychological wall between life and death</span>
  <span id="freeText7305715742817420300" style="display:none">'The Wall', the lead story in this collection, introduces three political prisoners on the night prior to their execution. Through the gaze of an impartial doctor--seemingly there for the men's solace--their mental descent is charted in exquisite, often harrowing detail. And as the morning draws inexorably closer, the men cross the psychological wall between life and death, long before the first shot rings out.<br /><br />This brilliant snapshot of life in anguish is the perfect introduction to a collection of stories where the neurosis of the modern world is mirrored in the lives of the people that inhabit it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7305715742817420300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_938615522" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/938615522" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/938615522?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer938615522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9666686100117557558"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1496870175i/22961182._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>“I wanted my own words. But the ones I use have been dragged through I don't know how many consciences.”<br/>― Jean-Paul Sartre, <i>The Wall</i> <br/><br/>The focus of this review is on the book's title piece - <i>The Wall</i>. This existentialist story has the feel of a film shot in stark black and white; the prose is as hard boiled as it gets and is told in first-person. The opening scene takes place in a large bare room with white walls where the narrator, Pablo Ibbieta, a man we can visualize with a thin, chiseled face,</span>
  <span id="freeText9666686100117557558" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1496870175i/22961182._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>“I wanted my own words. But the ones I use have been dragged through I don't know how many consciences.”<br>― Jean-Paul Sartre, <i>The Wall</i> <br><br>The focus of this review is on the book's title piece - <i>The Wall</i>. This existentialist story has the feel of a film shot in stark black and white; the prose is as hard boiled as it gets and is told in first-person. The opening scene takes place in a large bare room with white walls where the narrator, Pablo Ibbieta, a man we can visualize with a thin, chiseled face, slick back hair and looking a bit like Albert Camus or Humphrey Bogart - a visualization in keeping with the tone of one of those 1940s black and white films - is interrogated, and, along with two other men, sentenced to be shot dead. The three condemned are taken to a cellar with bench and mats, a room shivering cold and without a trace of warmth or humanity. The story unfolds in this hard, dank, ugly cellar room. Absurdity and despair, anyone?<br><br>Sartre has us live through the evening and night with Pablo and the two other convicted men: Tom, who has a thick neck and is fat around the middle (Pablo imagines bullets or bayonets cutting into his flesh), and Juan, who is young and has done nothing, other than being the brother of someone wanted by the authorities. We watch as Pablo and Tom and Juan turn old and gray; we smell urine when Tom unconsciously wets his pants; we hear Tom speaking of men executed by being run over by trucks to save ammunition.<br><br>A doctor enters the room and offers cigarettes and asks if anyone wants a priest. No one answers. Pablo falls asleep and wakes, having no thought of death or fear - what he is confronting is nameless; his reaction is physical - his cheeks burn and his head aches. Meanwhile, the doctor, referred to as the Belgian by Pablo, takes Juan's pulse and writes in his notebook. All is clinical; all is calculating. The cold penetrates - the doctor looks blue. Pablo sees that he himself is drenched in sweat. Sartre has written philosophical works such as <i>Being and Nothingness</i> where he addresses the meaninglessness of life and the reality of death in conceptual terms but in this story his ideas are given flesh and blood.<br><br>The core of the tale is all three men dealing with their own death. Juan sobs. Tom talks so he can recognize himself, that is, talk as a way of anchoring his sense of self in the world. He says something is going to happen he doesn't understand: death is a blank for Tom. And also for Pablo, who observes how the doctor entered the cellar to watch bodies, bodies dying in agony while still alive.<br><br>Pablo remembers living as if immortal and reflects he spent his life counterfeiting eternity, although he missed nothing, he understood nothing. Meanwhile, Tom touches the wooden bench as if touching death. Now that Pablo is looking at things through the lens of death, objects appear less dense - several hours or several years are all the same when you have lost the illusion of being eternal. Pablo feels a horrible calm, a distance from his body; his feeling of being with his body is as if he is tied to an enormous vermin. Feeling your body as an enormous vermin - how disgusting and alienating. Just in case if you are wondering if this is existentialism, this is existentialism.<br><br>The Doctor lets everyone know it is 3:30. At the mention of the time, Juan loses it and become hysterical but Pablo simply wants to die cleanly. After some agonizing minutes, the guards come in and take away Tom and Juan. Pablo hears shots fired out in the yard and wants to scream, but simply grits his teeth and pushes his hands in his pockets to stay clean. What does it mean to die cleanly? We are not given anything more specific. <br><br>Pablo is taken to the first floor where he is given a chance to live by revealing the whereabouts of one Ramon Gris. What happens from this point offers a twist, a twist, that is, for a tale soaking in absurdity, dread, alienation and death. Please read <i>The Wall</i>. You will be chilled; you will have an existentialist experience, you just might laugh so hard at the end you will start to cry.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1496871968i/22961309.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="9666686100117557558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It's all around, under her thin back, it fills the skirt, you'd think it was poured in, and besides it jiggles." <br /><br />Hell is other people... other people who can't appreciate a nice jiggling booty!<br /><br />If Sartre was alive today, I'm sure he'd give that comment a high five or whatever the French existensialist equivalent of that is (maybe a quiet snooty glare?)<br /><br />In fact, how can you appreciate someone who is perhaps the biggest influence on rap music in this century, still 20 years after his death? That's why I exist!<br /><br />To further belabor the point, we have the well known example from Lil Jon and the Eastide Boys's hit with the following lyrics:<br /><br />"Let me see you get low\<br />you scared you scared \<br />Drop dat ass to the floor \ <br />you scared you scared \<br />Let me see you get low \<br />you scared you scared \<br />Albert Camus and Jean Paul Sartre differed in view of \ <br />the abserd the abserd"<br /><br />Unfortunently, the rap community felt that this rift between Camus and Sartre would result in yet another (possibly violent) dichotomizing of the rap world. To avoid this, the final lyics were changed, altering the course of the song tremendously, also resulting in the removal of 20 other verses leaving only the tale of a shorty getting krunk thus avoiding any deep philosophical discussion.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16670674287195814359">If I had to pick a single common theme in the five short stories compiled in this book it would have to be self-actualization. Of course Sartre's self-actualization not only differs from the eastern Atman and Buddhi, it damn near opposes them. <br/><br/>Each short story portrays the protagonist's confrontation with the utter absurdity of life; and each protagonist refuses the prepackaged gift-wrapped souls that they've been handed through choosing a divergent path of their own. Some through wreaking havoc</span>
  <span id="freeText16670674287195814359" style="display:none">If I had to pick a single common theme in the five short stories compiled in this book it would have to be self-actualization. Of course Sartre's self-actualization not only differs from the eastern Atman and Buddhi, it damn near opposes them. <br /><br />Each short story portrays the protagonist's confrontation with the utter absurdity of life; and each protagonist refuses the prepackaged gift-wrapped souls that they've been handed through choosing a divergent path of their own. Some through wreaking havoc, some through self-affirming intimacy.<br /><br />This is a great book, written with beautifully crafted, realistic characters. Though I don't always consider "Realistic" to be a favorable aspect of a story, it has clearly worked this time.</span>

<div id="review_2023372705" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2023372705" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2023372705?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2023372705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9161979852982236069">Sartre has applied his existential depth to perhaps the most basic of themes here, but it works. This collection of 5 short stories written in late 1940's are a worthy read for any Sartre fan. The first story 'The Wall' is set during the Spanish Civil War with Pablo, one of the prisoners, being the protagonist. The story is one about the absurdity of life and how Pablo coincidentally and accidentally causes one of his comrades to be caught even though Pablo was not aware of it at all. The second</span>
  <span id="freeText9161979852982236069" style="display:none">Sartre has applied his existential depth to perhaps the most basic of themes here, but it works. This collection of 5 short stories written in late 1940's are a worthy read for any Sartre fan. The first story 'The Wall' is set during the Spanish Civil War with Pablo, one of the prisoners, being the protagonist. The story is one about the absurdity of life and how Pablo coincidentally and accidentally causes one of his comrades to be caught even though Pablo was not aware of it at all. The second story 'The Room' is much more spookier and the stereotypical bourgeois values are criticised with a woman who chooses to share the absurd world of her psychopathic husband. 'Erostratus' again sees the protagonist reject the accepted values of society and decides to go against society by randomly shooting people on the street. There is an autobiographical element in this book too as the main character is very much portrayed as Sartre. The stories are demanding in terms of engaging the reader emotionally and thoughtfully, and are extremely rewarding if you are willing to open to your mind to Sartre's way of thinking of people, and of the world. Should have read in French though, as the translation on my copy wasn't the greatest. I also can't help thinking he was a better writer of non-fiction and essays than he was of fiction. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9161979852982236069" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=gallimard-and-related">gallimard-and-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3408502491" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7773488543949526579">The first (undoubtedly the best) gives its title to the collection. “The Wall” is an impeccable and gripping text of around thirty pages, which accounts for human reactions to an extreme situation of existence.<br/>The plot is set in the Spanish Civil War of 1936, between republicans and nationalists. Francoist troops took three Republicans, prisoner. After a botched interrogation, they are locked in the cellar of a hospital. A commander comes to tell them the sentence: they will be shot the next day</span>
  <span id="freeText7773488543949526579" style="display:none">The first (undoubtedly the best) gives its title to the collection. “The Wall” is an impeccable and gripping text of around thirty pages, which accounts for human reactions to an extreme situation of existence.<br />The plot is set in the Spanish Civil War of 1936, between republicans and nationalists. Francoist troops took three Republicans, prisoner. After a botched interrogation, they are locked in the cellar of a hospital. A commander comes to tell them the sentence: they will be shot the next day at dawn. A Belgian doctor is introduced to the prisoners to note their reactions to the idea of ​​death ...<br />From there, while imbuing his text with a nauseating atmosphere, Sartre manages to extract the pure ore of anguish. The condemned cannot escape their situation; they perceive their death in an almost material way. They are confined and stuck in a fixed attitude, which no longer relates to existence but the essence, because death, very close, will justify them ...<br />Sartre brilliantly exploits this extraordinary metaphor of the wall. The symbol then accesses a great philosophical power ... In the proper sense, it is the section of the wall against which the condemned is placed to adjust it and bring it down. The wall prevents the cannons of pointed rifles from escaping ... But figuratively, the wall is infinite, impenetrable; it is what prevents us from fleeing our existence, the present situation, which we have to face.<br />Death is just one of many cases. It is impossible to flee its existence, its freedom, and its responsibility, because an opaque wall, invisible, omnipresent, stops any attempt to escape. The impasse of existence will never allow us to escape. You can never run away from yourself.<br /><br />A great text.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7773488543949526579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_433517265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433517265" />
    <a title="Okla Elliott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10074154-okla-elliott"><img alt="Okla Elliott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339138636p2/10074154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/433517265?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Okla Elliott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Okla Elliott" href="/user/show/10074154-okla-elliott">Okla Elliott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782070368785" href="/book/show/770661.Le_mur">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer433517265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3259023899074599153">Each of the pieces in this collection of short fiction has much to recommend it, but the final novella "Childhood of a Leader" stands out as one of finest novellas I've ever read. Even though the title story "The Wall" is considerably more famous, "Childhood of a Leader" is more ambitious and more groundbreaking. Written in the 1930s, this piece explores gender, class, sexuality, homo-eroticism, antisemitism, social-constructedness, and several key philosophical issues -- though it does all this</span>
  <span id="freeText3259023899074599153" style="display:none">Each of the pieces in this collection of short fiction has much to recommend it, but the final novella "Childhood of a Leader" stands out as one of finest novellas I've ever read. Even though the title story "The Wall" is considerably more famous, "Childhood of a Leader" is more ambitious and more groundbreaking. Written in the 1930s, this piece explores gender, class, sexuality, homo-eroticism, antisemitism, social-constructedness, and several key philosophical issues -- though it does all this in a prose that at once lyric and breezy. You can turn any page at random and find at least one passage that is beautiful merely on the level of sentence structure, imagery, or turn of phrase. I haven't been this impressed with a piece of short fiction in quite a while.<br /><br />[Side note: I read this collection several years ago when I was in undergrad, and I am now re-reading it for my PhD exams. I fell in love with the entire book back then, and I am enamored with it now, but interestingly, there are only a small handful of overlaps between my previous love and my current one. Of course, we change as we age, and that obviously explains this, but my point is that there was something for me in this book when I was in early twenties and even more now in my early thirties. Not many books age so well.]</span>
  <a data-text-id="3259023899074599153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_463035254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/463035254" />
    <a title="Arlina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2324635-arlina"><img alt="Arlina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394752147p2/2324635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/463035254?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arlina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arlina" href="/user/show/2324635-arlina">Arlina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer463035254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3631364891213455803">Damp hands creeping all over my body; that would be the sensation accompanying me during the read of “The Wall” by Sartre. In fiction, I don’t mind an atmosphere that is oppressive, helpless, absurd or anxious, as is the case in this short story collection. The curious side of me is more often than not bewitched by the subject of human unhappiness. Unhappiness that stems from a realization, that our own existence has a pettiness to it. <br/><br/>Even so, these stories weren’t able to truly fascinate me on</span>
  <span id="freeText3631364891213455803" style="display:none">Damp hands creeping all over my body; that would be the sensation accompanying me during the read of “The Wall” by Sartre. In fiction, I don’t mind an atmosphere that is oppressive, helpless, absurd or anxious, as is the case in this short story collection. The curious side of me is more often than not bewitched by the subject of human unhappiness. Unhappiness that stems from a realization, that our own existence has a pettiness to it. <br /><br />Even so, these stories weren’t able to truly fascinate me on a deeper level. I can appreciate them, especially having Sartre’s existentialist ideas in the back of my head while reading them, they just lacked a grab-you-by-the-throat factor for me. Another thing I noticed was the difference in quality among the stories; some were truly great, some just ‘okay’. I felt like the ‘okay’ stories did not have much backbone when stripped of the philosophical pre-knowledge at hand when reading them.<br /><br />A great thinker, a pretty good writer. <br /><br />3.5/5<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3631364891213455803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_74566272" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74566272" />
    <a title="Alexei" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2839899-alexei"><img alt="Alexei" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74566272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer604061152862851507">If all five short stories, included in this book, have such level of mastery and strength as titular story this would be one of the most splendid examples of literature. But, sadly, this collection of short stories is rather inconsistent. "The Wall" itself ia a haunting travel into inner life and experience of a man condemned to death, brilliant in its depiction of the smallest impulses of human psyche in this extreme (of course, borderline in existentialist meaning) situation and striking in it</span>
  <span id="freeText604061152862851507" style="display:none">If all five short stories, included in this book, have such level of mastery and strength as titular story this would be one of the most splendid examples of literature. But, sadly, this collection of short stories is rather inconsistent. "The Wall" itself ia a haunting travel into inner life and experience of a man condemned to death, brilliant in its depiction of the smallest impulses of human psyche in this extreme (of course, borderline in existentialist meaning) situation and striking in its furious attempts to understand (or maybe to approximate) the meaning of nonexistence. It's simply a masterpiece of philosophical literature. This story hasn't lingering philosophical meditations of "Nausea", but with all its strictly "belletristic" characteristics it catches true essence of Sartre's existentialist philosophy even better. Then comes "The Room" which is a kind of morbid and dark tale of insanity, understanding and love. It is very interesting and by no means bad, but it lacks the depth of "The Wall". If that was the end of this short stories' collection my rating would be obviously five out of five. But this book has three more stories. "Erostratus" is not very convincing story of a preoccupied with sexual deviations of violent nature misanthrope intended to kill six random people, "Intimacy" is simply meaningless and awful history of sexual life of a frigid (not exactly - she can obtain sexual pleasure but only through masturbation) woman with her impotent husband and her doubts of whether must she leave her husband for her macho-like lover or not (yes, it can be mistaken for some kind of a strange chick-lit, and this sums it all up). The final short story ("The Childhood of a Leader") is better than these two, but the depiction of protagonist's thoughts as child is unpersuasive and even primitive. It has good moments and nontrivial thoughts thrown in but not enough to the point of being masterpiece. <br />All in all, this book is a rather mixed bag but the fact that "The Room" obviously deserves a read and titular story is a work of genius secures this book's place in the "outstanding work of fiction" category.</span>
  <a data-text-id="604061152862851507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2601515708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer11056221402859181361">Fourth cover<br/>- What are they called, these three?<br/>"Steinbock, Ibbieta, and Mirbal," said the guardian.<br/>The commander put on his glasses and looked at his list:<br/>- Steinbock ... Steinbock ... That's it. You are sentenced to death.<br/>You will be shot tomorrow morning.<br/>He looked again:<br/>"The other two, too," he said.<br/>"It's not possible," said Juan. Not me.<br/>The captain looked at him with astonishment ... "<br/><br/>The Wall is a collection of five captivating news, but at the same time repulsive, even disgusting.<br/>The Wall </span>
  <span id="freeText11056221402859181361" style="display:none">Fourth cover<br />- What are they called, these three?<br />"Steinbock, Ibbieta, and Mirbal," said the guardian.<br />The commander put on his glasses and looked at his list:<br />- Steinbock ... Steinbock ... That's it. You are sentenced to death.<br />You will be shot tomorrow morning.<br />He looked again:<br />"The other two, too," he said.<br />"It's not possible," said Juan. Not me.<br />The captain looked at him with astonishment ... "<br /><br />The Wall is a collection of five captivating news, but at the same time repulsive, even disgusting.<br />The Wall is in my opinion the best news of the lot. The agonizing vanity of life and death of an absurd existence devoid of meaning is shown in all that it can contain viscous, sticky, unpleasantly odorous and ugly. The irony of the transposition of the fate which seems to weigh imminently on one person to another by an arbitrary tour de force of destiny has something really tragic and sublime.<br />The room and Erostate then drag us into the monstrous whirlwinds of unreason from the outside and the inside. One feels there the mysterious and frightening gulf that constitutes the possibility of the death of the spirit.<br /><br />Intimacy has been less of a challenge to me. The torments of a woman whose needs are filled in a way condemned by a prominent personality of his entourage are however very well shown by Sartre. If we compare with the news that surrounds, the discomfort is located in a frankly superficial horizon.<br /><br />Finally, given the context of its release in 1939, the latest news, The Childhood of a Leader, where the teenager gets out of his misery to a large extent by embracing anti-Semitism, leaves me a particularly acrid taste.<br /><br />Everyone knows that Sartre will contribute actively to the weekly collaborationist Comoedia and that he will arrange to cooperate with the Nazis to play Les Mouches in 1943 and Huis Clos in 1944. Although he has subtly shown his opposition to the Nazi regime it will not be in such a way as to jeopardize his career, but probably to ensure that he does not get too wet in order to preserve impunity in case the situation changes. "We have never been as free as under the German occupation," said Jean-Paul Sartre in The Republic of Silence. This narcissistic amoralism, this sluggishness in the face of horror, which undoubtedly contributed to his being nobelised in 1964, have always prevented me from estimating Sartre's person despite his undeniable qualities of playwright.<br /><br />Although subjects vary, these variations revolve around the same tone of existence: discomfort. discomfort in the face of death, then in front of madness in two different forms, in the life of a couple and finally during adolescence.<br /><br />Another remarkable element, the variation of the sexuality of the main character alternates from one news to another.<br /><br />Literally, I really enjoyed this collection that I devoured very quickly. It's really very good Sartre.</span>

The final story is simply amazing, following a young philosopher—much like Jean-Paul’s autobiography—learning the symbols of existence only to trade and trade again upon prevailing ideologies. The result is a lesson for our time.</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14648002696626566495">280918: i think i have read some of these before- decades... ago, mostly forgotten, now having read much more existentialism, philosophy, Sartre, and of course many other books, but my judgement is only further defined. this is great work. this also exemplifies the difficult union of philosophy and literature. in reading as probably in writing, there is a constant indeterminacy, a slippage, a refusal, to be one or the other....<br/><br/>as i read a lot, and a lot of philosophy, i remain convinced as an ar</span>
  <span id="freeText14648002696626566495" style="display:none">280918: i think i have read some of these before- decades... ago, mostly forgotten, now having read much more existentialism, philosophy, Sartre, and of course many other books, but my judgement is only further defined. this is great work. this also exemplifies the difficult union of philosophy and literature. in reading as probably in writing, there is a constant indeterminacy, a slippage, a refusal, to be one or the other....<br /><br />as i read a lot, and a lot of philosophy, i remain convinced as an artist that literature as any art has a different access or expression of truths of human worlds, than any other mode of thought. even abstract art must refer to the human, as implicit is the assertion that there is meaning, there are reasons to look at it, that this meaning is for humans to divine. this writing is far from abstract, though not from systems of meaning eg. freud, with great focus in these stories on concrete material experience, from what senses offer thoughts, from what emotions rise, tending towards causality that always returns to ambiguity. philosophy to art...<br /><br />this is the definition of ‘engaged writing’ that Sartre promotes, in that in writing it is not poetics, not metaphors, not metonymy, but ultimately political, philosophical, attitudes that must be depicted, must be morally engaged, so this is not beautiful writing in translation, this is somewhat stereotypical characters defined in the usual Marxist, class form, struggling with that inarguable human freedom argued for in his philosophy, so perhaps this fails to satisfy as either genre, but in history, in thought, this is a noble (Nobel) try...</span>
  <a data-text-id="14648002696626566495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4602765329020004495">3.75 stars<br/><br/>I first read and found his memoir entitled <b>Words</b> (Penguin, 2000) in 2014 tolerably enjoyable due to my unfamiliarity with his writing style, that is, page after page of lengthy narratives with few dialogs. Another reason is that I have regarded him and his works with awe and trepidation since, as a matter of course, he has been eminently acclaimed as one of the great philosophers in the 20th century (<a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre'>https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Pa...</a>). As for me, I have merely been a common read</span>
  <span id="freeText4602765329020004495" style="display:none">3.75 stars<br /><br />I first read and found his memoir entitled <b>Words</b> (Penguin, 2000) in 2014 tolerably enjoyable due to my unfamiliarity with his writing style, that is, page after page of lengthy narratives with few dialogs. Another reason is that I have regarded him and his works with awe and trepidation since, as a matter of course, he has been eminently acclaimed as one of the great philosophers in the 20th century (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Pa...</a>). As for me, I have merely been a common reader interested in practical philosophy as acquired in some foundation courses in my college/university years decades ago; therefore, I have my own respect to those having study philosophy and regard philosophical works as sublime (like a quote by John Milton printed on an Everyman's Library frontispiece, to verify and add later) in which, I think, we should be taught and guided by those who know or have studied and graduated in the field so that we should not go astray. <br /><br />Having recently got a copy of his 178-page <b>Nausea</b> (New Directions, 1969) since the middle of last month, I vaguely wondered if I had a heart on reading this book, his earliest novel, with relative pleasure and understanding so I planned to have a go with this 5-story collection first just to acquire some kind of literary immunity so that I can feel it's all right to read him in future, in other words, I've taken it as a foundation reading before plunging into the pool, his other lengthy novels like this one, <b>The Age of Reason</b> (1945), <b>The Reprieve</b> (1945), etc. <br /><br />There are five stories in this paperback: The Wall, The Room, Erostratus, Intimacy, and The Childhood of a Leader. Reading their synopses from this Wikipedia website: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Wall_(short_story_collection)">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Wal...</a> is a good idea in that each should serve as an overview prior to our exploration. Before reading this book, I couldn't help wondering why the fourth one has been inserted within the brackets in the book title, that is, <b>The Wall (Intimacy) And Other Stories.</b> However, this information gave me the light: "An earlier clothbound edition of this collection was published under the title <i>Intimacy."</i> (back cover) Eventually, I found reading these stories arguably enjoyable, especially <b>The Wall,</b> in which I liked most due to its inhuman setting, unique plot, astonishing climax, etc. regarding the fate of a political prisoner. As for the rest, I think they are fine but I refrained from rating this collection for its +.25 totaling its 4-star rating or higher due to the fifth story depicting a scene on a brief seemingly erotic relationships till I wondered if it's been portrayed as a typical young man having faith in or becoming a fledging follower of existentialism. <br /><br />To continue . . .</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12387784900745779462">Overall I am very impressed with how much Sartre can accomplish in so few pages in terms of story telling. A great and clear introduction, an intriguing climax then a surprise ending and a very relatable perspective to a characters world view under very strange circumstances during the whole time...<br/><br/>The Wall: I am quite impressed with the way the author describes the physical state of characters. I had to stop a few moments to think about how they feel, and how I would if I were in their shoes. A</span>
  <span id="freeText12387784900745779462" style="display:none">Overall I am very impressed with how much Sartre can accomplish in so few pages in terms of story telling. A great and clear introduction, an intriguing climax then a surprise ending and a very relatable perspective to a characters world view under very strange circumstances during the whole time...<br /><br />The Wall: I am quite impressed with the way the author describes the physical state of characters. I had to stop a few moments to think about how they feel, and how I would if I were in their shoes. A remarkable way of describing an unthinkable situation. The finish with a twist was great.<br /><br />The Room: I knew little(and still do probably) about Sartre's life at the time of reading it. Makes more sense after learning about the hallucinations he suffered.<br /><br />Erostratus: A great perspective to many of the psychos out there who go around killing people. <br /><br />Intimacy: I am not that big of a fan stream of consciousness and unconventional narration methods. The perspective of the narrator is a little convoluted but still the organization makes it understandable. <br /><br />Childhood of a Leader: Not knowing the age of the main chacter makes it a bit confusing... An interesting take on adoloscense.<br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4365717439968942136">Sartre delivers an invigorating short story collection about self-discovery and the nature of the self that is apt to change. A human being is always haunted by perplexing questions leading him nowhere. Realizing the absurdity of his own reality in this world, he is subject to different manifestations protruding unconscious actions which make him a social outcast in a communal well bonding society. Power, sexual repression, search for authenticity, and insanity magnify the book and constitute on</span>
  <span id="freeText4365717439968942136" style="display:none">Sartre delivers an invigorating short story collection about self-discovery and the nature of the self that is apt to change. A human being is always haunted by perplexing questions leading him nowhere. Realizing the absurdity of his own reality in this world, he is subject to different manifestations protruding unconscious actions which make him a social outcast in a communal well bonding society. Power, sexual repression, search for authenticity, and insanity magnify the book and constitute one of my favourite analytical reads.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4365717439968942136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3173746080" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3173746080" />
    <a title="Sajid Ahmed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/88691269-sajid-ahmed"><img alt="Sajid Ahmed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601665076p2/88691269.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3173746080?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sajid Ahmed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sajid Ahmed" href="/user/show/88691269-sajid-ahmed">Sajid Ahmed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3173746080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17700307150166421770">This collection of five short stories is supposed to be centered around the ways in which love (or at least lust) can corrupt one from an authentic life to an inauthentic one. However, the best story in the collection, THE WALL, seems to have much less to do with the corruption of love than a sort of cosmic joke being played on a guy whose just trying to save his life without harming another.<br/><br/>I comment a bit below on each story.<br/><br/>INTIMACY pp. 3-40<br/><br/>Lulu is married to Henri and they seem to be both in</span>
  <span id="freeText17700307150166421770" style="display:none">This collection of five short stories is supposed to be centered around the ways in which love (or at least lust) can corrupt one from an authentic life to an inauthentic one. However, the best story in the collection, THE WALL, seems to have much less to do with the corruption of love than a sort of cosmic joke being played on a guy whose just trying to save his life without harming another.<br /><br />I comment a bit below on each story.<br /><br />INTIMACY pp. 3-40<br /><br />Lulu is married to Henri and they seem to be both in love and not in love, alternatively attracting and repelling each other. Lulu does have a “part-time” lover, Pierre, and a girl friend, Rirette, in whom she confides.<br /><br />Finally she decides to leave Henri for Pierre and they will run away to Nice and have this ideal life. But she knows that is a myth, and ends up staying in the unsatisfying relationship with Henri, sort of preferring the intimacy she has, with all its problems, to the possibility of a better intimacy which she can’t quite believe in.<br /><br />EROSTRATUS pp. 41-58<br /><br />In ancient Greece Erostratus burned down an important temple to get recognition. He succeeded since no one knows who BUILT the temple, yet history knows who destroyed it.<br /><br />A modern days Parisian Erostratus plans to kill five randomly chosen strangers and then to run back home to hide. He knows he will be found out and the last bullet in his 6 chambered gun will be to kill himself.<br /><br />The narrator/would-be-killer is bitter. He makes his plans for these murders without any emotion. He could just as well be planning what to have for supper or what to listen to on the radio.<br /><br />However, I did find a curious oddity, if not tension within Sartre’s notion of authenticity – the choosing of one’s own life. When Sartre wants to demonstrate this freedom to choose one’s own values and life, it seems to never occur to him that in that exact mode: choosing one’s own values, in a world of where values are not objective but created by the individual, there is no guarantee what they will be. Just as the narrator of this story happens to choose a set of values that are radically contrary to typical social norms, one could well follow Sartre’s general rule of creating one’s own values, and happen to choose values which are well loved by society in general.<br /><br />I think of the character Dr. Rioux in Albert Camus’ THE PLAGUE. He seems to me to be a very free man of the Sartrean sort. Yet, his response to the plague which is killing everyone in Oman, and will likely kill him if he tends to his sick patients, is to go right on treating them. Not because he is, in Sartre’s sense, inauthentic and choosing societal values or values not his own, but PRECISELY because being a doctor and treating people even in the face of likely death, IS his authentic value. I don’t think Sartre can quite deal with this fact of the possibility of authentic values, for some, being similar to everyday common values.<br /><br />THE WALL p.59-80<br /><br />I do love the way Sartre writes, and his humor. In this case I was delighted with one quiet joke. When a prisoner reveals that the Spanish military at times kill people by running over them with trucks in order to save ammunition, the Sartre-like figure replies: “But, it costs them petrol!” I like that sort of savage out-side-the-box humor which Sartre often uses.<br /><br />All the prisoners are going to die and they experience the expectation -- “tomorrow life is no longer possible.”<br /><br />“In the state I was in, if someone had come and told me I could go home quietly, that they would leave me my life whole, it would have left me cold: several hours or several years of waiting is all the same when you have lost the illusion of being eternal.”<br /><br />This seem nonsense to me. Things happen in the world we can’t imagine and have no control over. Like, for example, Gris being found in the cemetery as the prisoner had made up! We simply live every day with our mortality.<br /><br />THE CHILDHOOD OF A LEADER pp. 81-159<br /><br />This is a long and rather sad story of Lucian Fleurier from his birth until his adulthood. This rather unlikeable young man was a bit of a terror as a child, flirting seriously with suicide and often destructive. But, at least in that period he was mainly himself. However, as he entered adolescence and young adulthood he became increasingly influenced by others, manipulated by them, giving up his own freedom to the will of those others. He was easily impressed and wanted acceptance.<br /><br />It seems a classic case of Sartre’s notion of inauthenticity or bad faith. However, it is important to note that it isn’t the CONTENT of his decisions that constitute his bad faith, but the SOURCE of his decisions which seem to rest in pleasing others and trying to adapt his life to their expectations and hopes for him. He could have come to the same life conclusions in good faith had he made the choice on his own strength, rather than because of the pressure of others and his desire to please.<br /><br />THE ROOM pp.160-189<br /><br />This is a fascinating allegory of the “real” world versus the world of the existentialism. At least that’s how I read it. A young woman has married a man who goes complete mad. Her parents, representing the “normal” world, expect that she will put him away and get on with her life. But, much to their dismay, she enters into the world of her husband, who is absolutely crazy, and defends keeping him home, caring for him at the expense of her life, and acts as though the others, beyond that wall (there’s more that one wall going on here) don’t exist.<br /><br />Little by little she is drawn into his world, but not completely. Yet there are strong hints that perhaps, just perhaps, she will. However, if the outside world, the world beyond the wall, decided to take him by force to an asylum, she is prepared to kill him first to protect him from losing his freedom.<br /><br />He lives in one room, never wanting to leave, separated from the rest of the world by “the wall.” The husband describes it:<br /><br />Normal people think I belong with them. But I couldn’t stay an hour among them. I need to live out there in the other side of the wall. But they don’t want me out there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17700307150166421770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

He works out his existentialist ideas into real-world situations. All stories are concerned with identity, responsibility, freedom, existence, living in bad faith with yourself and others. But I guess this is not really my cup of tea.</span>

<div id="review_2827768819" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2827768819" />
    <a title="Jelle Rijntjes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90335669-jelle-rijntjes"><img alt="Jelle Rijntjes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544351692p2/90335669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2827768819?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jelle Rijntjes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jelle Rijntjes" href="/user/show/90335669-jelle-rijntjes">Jelle Rijntjes</a>

        rated it

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2827768819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7806133409176297787">It's amazing how many depths Sartre can create in a 40 (+-) page story.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3515010966" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3515010966" />
    <a title="Sam Dobbs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99921189-sam-dobbs"><img alt="Sam Dobbs" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3515010966?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Dobbs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Dobbs" href="/user/show/99921189-sam-dobbs">Sam Dobbs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3515010966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8399440178959564617">Very, very good. Probably even better if you have a deep understanding of existentialism. I only have a basic understanding, but I still enjoyed those themes which I did recognise, they were explored beautifully. Each story had a very individual tone, very impressive to be able to write stories with such disparate styles. All in all, good good good good</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1167807484" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1167807484" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/281393-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576398338p2/281393.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1167807484?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/281393-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1167807484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4868338906107051219">This book was a total surprise to me. I didn’t know it existed. I read <i>Nausea</i> when I was about nineteen and watched the TV adaptation of <i>The Roads to Freedom</i> so I didn’t have to read the novels and somewhere along the line I saw <i>No Exit</i> (probably the TV version with Harold Pinter) but that was me. And then one day I ran across this wee gem: thank you Internet. Okay, it’s dated, but Jane Austen’s dated; you work round it. There wasn’t a single bad piece here although I’m not sure I’d want to rank</span>
  <span id="freeText4868338906107051219" style="display:none">This book was a total surprise to me. I didn’t know it existed. I read <i>Nausea</i> when I was about nineteen and watched the TV adaptation of <i>The Roads to Freedom</i> so I didn’t have to read the novels and somewhere along the line I saw <i>No Exit</i> (probably the TV version with Harold Pinter) but that was me. And then one day I ran across this wee gem: thank you Internet. Okay, it’s dated, but Jane Austen’s dated; you work round it. There wasn’t a single bad piece here although I’m not sure I’d want to rank them and I suspect a second read wouldn’t hurt either especially it I can find a copy with bigger print. <br /><br />The only thing I can compare ‘The Wall’ to is Victor Hugo’s <i>The Last Day of a Condemned Man</i> and maybe the end of <i>The Outsider</i>. (All Frenchmen—interesting.) Sartre’s tale has a nice twist which I didn’t expect from him at all. ‘The Room’—at least the second part—has a truly Gothic feel to it and probably was my favourite of the five pieces; a very uncomfortable read. ‘Erostratus’ poses an interesting question: <blockquote>        "I know your character," he said to me. "His name is Erostratus. He wanted to become famous and he couldn't find anything better to do than to bum down the temple of Ephesus, one of the seven wonders of the world."<br />        "And what was the name of the man who built the temple?"<br />        "I don't remember," he confessed. "1 don't believe anybody mows his name."<br />         “But you remember the name of Erostratus'? You see, he didn't things out too badly." </blockquote>In our fame-obsessed world—kids don’t’ want to be train drivers or air hostesses nowadays, they just want to be famous—how many people have done something similar simply to ensure they get their full fifteen minutes?<br /><br />‘Intimacy’ was probably my least favourite story and mainly because it was dated. "She is my wife," he said, "she belongs to me, I want her to come back with me." Who says stuff like that these days? Well, in the west, at least. But it’s interesting to see Sartre write from a woman’s point of view.<br /><br />The last piece, the novella <i>The Childhood of a Leader</i>, is probably the best of the five pieces. It traces a young boy from the age of about four to young adulthood and is striking in its analysis. He’s the son of a rich industrialist and realises early on that one day he’s going to have to take over the family business but he’s a lot of growing up to do beforehand and that’s not going to be easy because it’s obvious he’s a disturbed individual. His mother dressing him up as a little girl certainly didn’t help and his sexuality is one thing he struggles with. But he’s also, as the Chinese are purported to say, living in interesting times. The world’s changing and is full of new ideas, new philosophies, psychoanalysis, changing political and sexual notions. This is the gauntlet he has to run before he’ll ever become a leader. It’s also what shapes (deforms) him over the years. The story’s main weakness (although it’s a minor point) is Sartre doesn’t make it clear what age the boy is. Maybe a French person living at the time would be able to work it out with ease but I struggled. <br /><br />Overall a dark collection. No one’s very happy at least not for any length of time. The man can certainly write but I think I prefer Camus overall. Saying that if someone unearthed an unpublished collection or a novel I’d still be keen to read it. Not in too great a rush to read his philosophy in the raw though. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4868338906107051219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RFgANOq22e1iLSsFMroGMVPahHWn/PWfWtqnqGZp/E4ADAynN9CyML0PcvsT+P0aeKGr2mw8O5CJERcFHivnCQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('10smUJPyS1gUb5s5hxFNJeAVx0HPHTCOYRCtelb4kcGTHyrDTpQghctNwsemU7YOy27o7gTd/oGy2x3XLrqKhg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1466.Jean_Paul_Sartre">About Jean-Paul Sartre</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16693390022238039969"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br/><br/>He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has ex</span>
  <span id="freeText16693390022238039969" style="display:none"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br /><br />He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has exerted a far-reaching influence on our age."<br /><br />In the years around the time of his death, however, existentialism declined in French philosophy and was overtaken by structuralism, represented by Levi-Strauss and, one of Sartre's detractors, Michel Foucault.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16693390022238039969" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
